Ninth Proof: Angels as Witnesses to God's Majesty
Razi argues that angels are uniquely suited to demonstrate God's greatness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses Quranic depictions of angels to argue that they uniquely manifest God's greatness, outweighing any other creation.

Core Idea
Angels are the ultimate witnesses to God’s majesty, as chosen by God Himself.

In this passage, Razi presents his ninth proof by analyzing a Quranic scene of Judgment Day where angels stand in awe. He argues that if any other being were more fit to demonstrate God's greatness, they would be mentioned instead. This shows angels hold a supreme position in manifesting divine magnificence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the two Quranic verses cited and the logical flow of Razi's argument.
The verses are from Surat an-Naba (78) and Surat az-Zumar (75).
2
Identify the two verses
Find the two verses: the first describes angels standing in rows on Judgment Day; the second shows them surrounding the Throne in this world.
The term 'Spirit' (الروح) is often interpreted as a special angel or Jibril.
3
Understand Razi's core argument
Razi states the purpose of these scenes is to emphasize God's greatness. He then argues that if any other group were better suited, they would have been mentioned. Since angels are mentioned, they are the most fitting.
This is a 'conditional argument' based on divine selection.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Razi concludes that angels have no comparison to humans in indicating God's majesty, both in the Hereafter and in this world.
This reinforces the high status of angels in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة : قوله تعالى: ﴿وَيَوْمَ يَقُومُ الرُّوحُ وَالْمَلَائِكَةُ صَفًّا لَّا يَتَكَلَّمُونَ إِلَّا مَنْ أَذِنَ لَهُ الرَّحْمَنُ وَقَالَ صَوَابًا﴾ [النبأ : ۳۸] والمقصود من شرح هذه الواقعة : المبالغة في شرح عظمة الله ولو كان في الخلق طائفة قيامهم بين يدي الله تعالى وتضرعهم في حضرة الله أقوى في الإنباء عن عظمة الله وكبريائه من الملائكة لكان ذكرهم في هذا المقام أولى، ثم إنه سبحانه كما بين عظمته في الدار الآخرة بذكر الملائكة فكذلك بين عظمته في الدار الدنيا بذكر الملائكة، قال تعالى : ﴿وَتَرَى الْمَلَائِكَةَ حَافِّينَ مِنْ حَوْلِ الْعَرْشِ يُسَبِّحُونَ بِحَمْدِ رَبِّهِمْ﴾ [الزمر : ٧٥] وهذا يدل على أنه لا نسبة لهم إلى البشر البتة.
The ninth proof: His statement, exalted: 'And the day the Spirit and the angels stand in rows, they will not speak except one whom the Most Merciful permits and who speaks what is right' [Naba:78]. The purpose of explaining this scene is to exaggerate in explaining the greatness of God. Had there been in creation a group whose standing before God and their supplication in God's presence were stronger in indicating God's greatness and majesty than the angels, their mention would have been more appropriate in this context. Then He, glorified, just as He made known His greatness in the Hereafter by mentioning the angels, likewise He made known His greatness in the present world by mentioning the angels. He said, exalted: 'And you see the angels surrounding the Throne, exalting with praise of their Lord' [Zumar:75]. This indicates that there is no comparison whatsoever between them and humans.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage presents the ninth proof based on a Quranic scene of Judgment Day where the Spirit and angels stand in rows. Razi argues that the purpose of describing this scene is to emphasize God's greatness. If any other group (including humans) were better suited to illustrate God's majesty, they would have been mentioned instead of angels. Since angels are chosen, they are the most fitting. Additionally, God also describes angels in this world surrounding the Throne, indicating their unique status. Razi concludes that angels have no comparison to humans.

Key Terms
روح
صف
عرش
عظمة الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The absence of humans in the scene means humans are not present on that day.
Correction: Razi argues the mention indicates angels are more fitting, not that humans are absent.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's ninth proof, why does the Quran mention angels standing in rows on Judgment Day rather than humans?
A) Because humans are not present on that day.
B) Because angels are more capable of indicating God's majesty.
C) Because angels are more numerous.
D) Because humans cannot speak.

Key Takeaway
Razi's ninth proof shows that God specifically mentions angels in scenes of majesty, proving their superior role in demonstrating His greatness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that angels stand in rows, silent except by God's permission, does your own speech reflect that same reverence? In what area of your life do you speak without first seeking His permission?
?
If God chose angels—not humans—to most powerfully display His majesty, what does that demand of your humility? Are you striving to be among those who acknowledge His greatness, or are you content with pride?
?
The angels surround the Throne constantly glorifying God. When was the last time you paused to reflect on God's greatness as revealed in these scenes? How can you make that reflection a daily practice that transforms your worship?

The Tenth Proof: Angels as Guardians over Prophets
Razi cites a Quranic verse and argues its generality proves angel superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses the generality of the Quranic verse about guardian angels to prove the superiority of angels over prophets.

Core Idea
Guardian angels over prophets prove angelic superiority.

Imam Razi concludes his series of proofs for the superiority of angels over prophets with a direct Quranic argument. The verse from Surah al-Infitar states that every human has noble guardian angels recording deeds. Since this applies to all morally responsible beings, including prophets, the fact that angels are appointed as guardians over the best of humanity powerfully indicates their higher rank.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and its context
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice that Razi quotes only a portion of the verse, focusing on the universal guardianship.
The verse is from Surah al-Infitar, verses 10-11.
2
Identify the logical inference
Understand how Razi uses the generality of the verse: it applies to all morally responsible humans, including prophets. If angels are appointed as guardians over prophets, then the guardian must be of a higher rank than the one guarded.
This is a key principle in Islamic theology: the guardian (hafiz) is superior.
3
Address the potential misunderstanding
Reflect on why guardianship might seem to imply lower status. In Islamic thought, however, the guardian is entrusted with a role over the guarded, indicating superiority, not inferiority.
This clarifies a common confusion about the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة العاشرة : قوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِنَّ عَلَيْكُمْ لَحَافِظِينَ كِرَامًا كَاتِبِينَ﴾ [الانفطار : ۱۰ - ١١] وهذا عام في حق جميع المكلفين من بني آدم، فيدخل فيه الأنبياء وغيرهم.
The tenth proof: His statement, exalted: 'And indeed, over you are guardians, noble writers' [Infitar:10-11]. This is general with respect to all morally responsible beings from the children of Adam, so it includes prophets and others.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth and final proof. Razi cites the Quranic verse that states there are guardian angels over every human being. The verse is general and includes all morally responsible humans, which includes prophets. Since angels are appointed over prophets as guardians, it indicates that angels are superior in rank.

Key Terms
حافظون
كاتبون
مكلفون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Guardianship implies that the guardian is of lower status, like a servant protecting a master.
Correction: In Islamic theology, the role of guardian (حافظ) indicates a higher rank and responsibility over the one guarded. Thus, angels being guardians over prophets demonstrates angelic superiority.

Checkpoint
How does the verse about guardian angels (حافظين) support the superiority of angels over prophets?
A) It says angels are rewarded more.
B) It includes prophets under angelic guardianship.
C) It mentions angels before humans.
D) It says angels are sinless.

Key Takeaway
The generality of the verse on guardian angels proves that even prophets are under angelic guardianship, establishing the superiority of angels.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly accept that even the prophets, the best of creation, are under the watch of angels? How does that awareness affect your own sense of accountability before Allah, knowing that you too are under the same guardianship?
?
If angels are noble and superior in rank, are you living with the consciousness that they are present with you? Does this change how you behave when you are alone, or do you ignore their presence?
?
This proof humbles human pride by showing that angels outrank prophets. Are you willing to lower yourself before Allah's creation, accepting that your status is limited, and that your duty is to submit rather than to boast?

Two Proofs for Angels' Superiority Over Humans
Imam Razi gives two reasons and a Quranic evidence for angels' superiority over humans.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two logical reasons and the Quranic evidence that Imam Razi uses to argue for the superiority of angels over humans.

Core Idea
Angels are superior because they guard us from sin and their testimony is more reliable.

Imam Razi presents a concise argument for the superiority of angels over humans, using two rational proofs and a scriptural confirmation. This passage teaches us how to use logical reasoning in theological matters, showing that even in comparing human and angelic ranks, careful argumentation is required.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without translating. Notice the structure: two numbered reasons followed by a Quranic verse.
There is a garbled fragment in the middle; ignore it for now.
2
Understand the first reason
Identify why being a guardian necessitates being further from sin. Reflect on the logic: the guardian must be more perfect than the guarded in the relevant aspect.
3
Understand the second reason
Consider why the angels' recording being a proof for humans implies their word is more acceptable. This relates to the idea of testimony.
4
Analyze the Quranic evidence
Read the verse from Surah al-Jinn and see how it shows that prophets need angelic protection, indicating angels' superior status.
Note the consensus that 'observers' refers to angels.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا يقتضي كون الملائكة أفضل من البشر لوجهين.
الأول : أنه تعالى جعلهم حفظة لبني آدم والحافظ للمكلف - من المعصية - لا بد أن يكون أبعد عن الخطأ والمعصية من المحفوظ، وهذا يقتضي كونهم أبعد عن المعاصي وأقرب إلى الطاعات من البشر، وذلك يقتضي مزيد الفضل. الثاني : : أنه سبحانه وتعالى جعل كتابتهم حجة للبشر في الطاعة وحجة عليهم في المعاصي، وذلك يقتضي أن يكون قولهم أولى بالقبول من قول البشر، فلو كان لكان الأمر بالعكس.
البشر أعظم [؟] حالا منهم [؟] الملائكة،
ويقرب من هذا الدليل التمسك بقوله تعالى: ﴿عَلِمُ الْغَيْبِ فَلَا يُظْهِرُ عَلَى غَيْبِهِ أحَدًا إِلَّا مَنِ ارْتَضَى مِن رَّسُولٍ فَإِنَّهُ يَسْلُكُ مِنْ بَيْنِ يَدَيْهِ وَمِنْ خَلْفِهِ رَصَدًا لِيَعْلَمَ أَن قَدْ أَبْلَغُوا رِسَلَتِ رَبِّهِمْ ﴾ [الجن: ٢٦ - ٢٨] وأجمعوا على أن هذا الرصد هم وهذا يدل على أن الأنبياء - عليهم السلام - لا يصيرون مأمونين من التخليط في الوحي إلا بإعانة الملائكة وتقويتهم، وكل ذلك يدل على الفضل الظاهر.
And this necessitates that angels are superior to humans in two respects. First: that He, exalted, made them guardians for the children of Adam, and the guardian of the accountable person (from sin) must necessarily be further from error and sin than the one guarded, and this necessitates that they are further from sins and closer to acts of obedience than humans, and that necessitates additional merit. Second: that He, glorified and exalted, made their recording a proof for humans in obedience and a proof against them in sins, and that necessitates that their statement is more worthy of acceptance than the statement of humans, otherwise the matter would be the opposite. Humans are greater [؟] condition from them [؟] the angels. And close to this proof is adhering to His statement: 'He is the Knower of the Unseen, and He does not disclose His Unseen to anyone except whom He approves as a messenger, and then He sends before him and behind him observers to make evident that they have conveyed the messages of their Lord' [Quran 72:26-28]. And they unanimously agreed that these observers are the angels, and this indicates that the prophets, peace be upon them, are not secure from confusion in revelation except with the assistance of angels and their strengthening, and all of that indicates manifest merit.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage presents two logical reasons and a scriptural evidence for the superiority of angels over humans. It is a proof by Imam Razi. He begins by stating that the previous discussion necessitates that angels are better than humans in two respects. The first reason: God made angels guardians over humans. A guardian from sin must be further from error and sin than the one guarded, so angels are more obedient and thus more virtuous. The second reason: God made the angels' recording a proof for humans in obedience and against them in disobedience, so their testimony is more acceptable than humans'. Then he adds a supporting evidence from Quran 72:26-28, where God sends observers (angels) to protect the revelation, indicating angels are necessary for the prophets' protection from error. Thus angels have manifest merit.

Key Terms
الملائكة
الأفضل
الحافظ
الرصد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that human prophets are superior to angels because of their prophethood and closeness to God.
Correction: Imam Razi argues that the protective and witnessing roles of angels show their higher rank in terms of freedom from sin and reliability, even over prophets.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi, what is the first reason for angels being superior to humans?
A) Angels are longer-lived.
B) Angels are guardians over humans, requiring them to be further from sin.
C) Angels are more powerful.
D) Angels are creators.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi uses two logical arguments and a Quranic verse to demonstrate that angels are superior to humans in moral purity and reliability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you comfortable with the fact that angels are more trustworthy than you? What does that say about your own reliability in your duties to God and others? Will you take one step today to become more honest in a specific area of your life?
?
The prophets themselves needed angelic assistance to avoid error. Do you rely on the angels' protection from sin, or do you think you can manage on your own? What concrete step can you take today to seek their help in avoiding a specific sin you struggle with?
?
If you truly believed that angels are guarding you and recording your deeds as authoritative testimony, what would you do differently in the next hour? Will you change your actions or thoughts based on that awareness right now?

Precedence in Mention Indicates Honor
Imam Razi proves angels' superiority by showing God mentions angels before humans in several Quranic verses and uses custom and hadith to validate the principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses the principle of precedence in mention (al-taqdim fi al-dhikr) to argue for the superiority of angels over humans, based on Quranic verses, customary practice, and prophetic approval.

Core Idea
Precedence in mention reflects precedence in honor, both in custom and in revelation.

In this passage, Imam Razi presents his eleventh proof for the superiority of angels. He cites several Quranic verses where God mentions angels before humans, and argues that this order is not accidental. Through examples from poetry and the Treaty of Hudaybiyyah, he shows that custom treats precedence in mention as a mark of honor, and the Prophet's hadith confirms that what Muslims deem good is good with God. This lesson examines this principle and its application.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic evidence
The passage lists four verses where angels are mentioned before humans. Notice the order: God, angels, books, messengers in 2:285; God, angels, people of knowledge in 3:18; God and angels send blessings in 33:56; God chooses messengers from angels and people in 22:75.
Consider why the order is consistent.
2
Understand the principle
The core claim: precedence in mention indicates precedence in honor. Ask yourself: why would this be a valid argument?
3
Examine the custom examples
Imam Razi gives two examples from custom: the poet's line where 'gray hair' is placed before 'Islam', criticized by Umar, and the dispute over name order in the treaty. These show that custom recognizes precedence as honor.
4
Connect to hadith
The hadith 'What Muslims see as good is good with God' bridges custom and divine law, making the customary principle legally binding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الحادية عشرة : قوله تعالى : ءَامَنَ الرَّسُولُ بِمَا أُنزِلَ إِلَيْهِ مِن رَّبِّهِ، وَالْمُؤْمِنُونَ كل آمنَ بِاللَّهِ وَمَلَكَهِ، وَكُتُبِهِ وَرُسُلِهِ ﴾ [البَقَرَة : ۲۸۵] فبين تعالى أنه لا بد في صحة الإيمان بهذه الأشياء فبدأ بنفسه وثنى بملائكته وثلث بكتبه، وربع برسله، وكذا في قوله : وشَهِدَ اللهُ أَنَّهُ لا إلهَ إِلَّا هُوَ وَالْمَلَيْكَةُ وَأُولُوا العلم [آل عمران: ۱۸] وفي قوله : وإِنَّ اللهَ وَمَلَيْكَتَهُ يُصَلُّونَ عَلَى النَّبِيُّ ﴾ [الأحزاب: ٥٦] وقال الله تعالى : الله يصْطَفِي مِنَ الْمَلَكَةِ رُسُلًا وَمِنَ النَّاس [ الحج : ٧٥] . والتقديم في الذكر يدل على التقديم في الشرف، والدليل عليه أن على الأشرف في الذكر قبيح عرفًا، فوجب أن يكون قبيحا شرعاً ، أما أنه قبيح عرفا فلأن الشاعر لما قال : عميرة ودع إن تجهزت غازيا كفى الشيب والإسلام للمرء ناهيا قال عمر الله : " لو قدمت الإسلام لأعطيتك، ولما كتبوا كتاب الصلح بين رسول الله ل وبين المشركين وقع التنازع في تقديم الاسم وهذا يدل على أن التقديم في الذكر يدل على مزيد الشرف والفضل، وإذا ثبت أنه كذلك في العرف وجب أن يكون في الشرع كذلك، لقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : «ما رآه المسلمون حسنا فهو عند الله : حسن».
The eleventh proof: His statement: 'The Messenger has believed in what was revealed to him from his Lord, and the believers; each one believes in God, His angels, His books, and His messengers' [Quran 2:285]. He made clear that it is necessary for the correctness of faith in these things; He began with Himself, then with His angels, then with His books, then with His messengers. Similarly in His statement: 'God bears witness that there is no deity except Him, and the angels and those of knowledge' [Quran 3:18]. And in His statement: 'Indeed, God and His angels send blessings upon the Prophet' [Quran 33:56]. And God said: 'God chooses messengers from the angels and from people' [Quran 22:75]. And precedence in mention indicates precedence in honor; the evidence for it is that precedence of the more honorable in mention is considered objectionable customarily, so it must be objectionable legally. As for it being objectionable customarily, because when the poet said: 'O 'Umaira, if you prepare to go as a warrior, suffice it that gray hair and Islam are a prohibitor for a man,' 'Umar, may God be pleased with him, said: 'If you had given precedence to Islam, I would have given you [a reward].' And when they wrote the treaty between the Messenger of God, peace and blessings be upon him, and the polytheists, there occurred a dispute regarding the precedence of the name. And this indicates that precedence in mention indicates additional honor and merit. And if it is established that it is so in custom, then it must be so in law, due to his statement, peace and blessings be upon him: 'What the Muslims see as good is good in the sight of God.'
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage presents the eleventh proof for angels' superiority. Imam Razi cites several Quranic verses where God mentions angels before humans: in 2:285, God begins with Himself, then angels, then books, then messengers; in 3:18, God mentions Himself, then angels, then people of knowledge; in 33:56, God and His angels send blessings on the Prophet; in 22:75, God chooses messengers from angels and from people. The argument is that precedence in mention indicates precedence in honor (sharaf). This is supported by custom (urf): the poet was criticized for mentioning 'shayb' (gray hair) before 'Islam', and in the Treaty of Hudaybiyyah, there was a dispute over whose name to mention first. Since precedent in mention is understood in custom to indicate honor, and the Prophet said 'What Muslims see as good is good with God,' it is legally valid that God's precedence in mentioning angels shows their higher rank.

Key Terms
التقديم في الذكر
الشرف
العرف
الشرع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Precedence in mention is accidental or irrelevant to honor.
Correction: The author shows it is intentional, supported by customary practice and prophetic approval.

Checkpoint
What is the main evidence Imam Razi uses to show that precedence in mention indicates honor?
A) A logical syllogism.
B) Customary practice and a hadith.
C) Consensus of the scholars.
D) The biography of the Prophet.

Key Takeaway
Precedence in mention is a valid indicator of honor, based on custom and prophetic approval, and is used to prove angels' superiority over humans.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak about God's creation—angels, prophets, scholars—does the order of your words reflect your heart's true estimation of their rank? Will you examine your speech today to ensure you honor what God honors?
?
The poet almost lost a reward because he mentioned gray hair before Islam. Are there ‘gray hairs’ in your life—worldly concerns, habits, or relationships—that you place before your faith in your priorities? What will you reorder starting now?
?
Imagine you are writing your own ‘treaty’—a decision or commitment. Whose name will you put first? Is it God's name, or your own? What would it cost you to give God precedence in every intention and action?

The Teacher-Learner Principle and Intellectual Knowledge
Razi establishes the teacher-learner principle and classifies intellectual knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses the principle that a teacher must be more knowledgeable than the student to argue for angels' superiority, and how he divides intellectual knowledge into obligatory and non-obligatory.

Core Idea
The angel Jibril is more knowledgeable than the Prophet because he is the teacher.

Imam Razi presents his twelfth proof for angels' superiority by focusing on knowledge. He first establishes a simple logical premise: the teacher must know more than the student. Then he categorizes knowledge to show that in every category, Jibril excels. This lesson covers the first part of that argument, including the division of intellectual knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Razi begins with a direct claim and supports it with Quranic evidence and a classification of knowledge.
The Quranic reference 'Severe in strength' directly names Jibril as the teacher.
2
Identify the main premise
Razi states: 'The teacher must necessarily be more knowledgeable than the student.' Find this point and understand why it is the foundational premise of the proof.
3
Understand the division of intellectual knowledge
Razi divides intellectual knowledge into obligatory (knowledge of God's essence and attributes) and non-obligatory (details of creation). In which category does Jibril have an advantage?
The obligatory part is equal for angels and prophets, so the advantage lies in non-obligatory knowledge.
4
Explain why Jibril knows more non-obligatory matters
Razi argues that Jibril is longer-lived and has witnessed more of creation, so his knowledge of things like the Throne, angels, and animals is more abundant. Try to paraphrase this reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية عشرة : الملك أعلم من البشر، والأعلم أفضل.
إنما قلنا : إنه أعلم لأن جبريل كان معلما لمحمد صلى الله عليه وسلم بدليل قوله تعالى : ﴿شَدِيدُ الْقُوَى﴾ [النجم: ٥] والمعلم لا بد أن يكون أعلم من المتعلم.
وأيضًا : فالعلوم قسمان : عقلية ونقلية.
أما العقلية فمنها ما هو واجب كالعلم بذات الله وصفاته، ولا يجوز وقوع التقصير فيها للملائكة وللأنبياء، ومنها ما ليس بواجب، وهو كالعلم بكيفية مخلوقات الله وما فيها من العجائب كالعلم بأحوال العرش والكرسي واللوح والقلم والجنة والنار، وأطباق السماوات، وأصناف الملائكة، وأنواع الحيوانات في المفاوز والجبال والبحار، ولا شك أن جبريل أعرف بها [؟] لأنه أطول عمرا ، وأكثر مشاهدة لها، فكان علمه بها أكثر وأكمل.
وأما العلوم النقلية التي لا تعرف إلا بالوحي فهي لم تحصل لمحمد صلى الله عليه وسلم ولا لسائر الأنبياء إلا من جهة جبريل عليه السلام، فيستحيل أن يكون لمحمد فيها فضيلة على جبريل، وأما جبريل عليه السلام فإنه كان هو الواسطة بين الله تعالى وبين جميع الأنبياء، فهو عالم بكل الشرائع الماضية والحاضرة، وهو أيضًا عالم بشرائع الملائكة وتكاليفهم، ومحمد صلى الله عليه وسلم ما كان عالما بشيء من ذلك.
فثبت أن جبريل أعلم من محمد [؟] فوجب [؟] أن يكون أفضل منه، لقوله تعالى : ﴿هَلْ يَسْتَوِي الَّذِينَ يَعْلَمُونَ وَالَّذِينَ لَا يَعْلَمُونَ﴾ [الزمر: ٩] أقصى ما في الباب أن يقال: إن آدم علم الأسماء كلها ولم تعلمها الملائكة، ولكن الظاهر أن العلم بالحقائق والشرائع أفضل من العلم بالأسماء، فكان جبريل [؟] أفضل من آدم [؟].
The twelfth proof: The angel is more knowledgeable than humans, and the more knowledgeable is superior. We only say that he is more knowledgeable because Jibril was a teacher to Muhammad, peace and blessings be upon him, as evidence of His statement: 'Severe in strength' [Quran 53:5]. And the teacher must necessarily be more knowledgeable than the student. Also, knowledge is of two kinds: intellectual and transmitted. As for intellectual, some of it is obligatory, like knowledge of God's essence and attributes, and deficiency in it is not permissible for angels or prophets. And some of it is not obligatory, like knowledge of the manner of God's creations and what is in them of wonders, such as knowledge of the conditions of the Throne, the Footstool, the Preserved Tablet, the Pen, Paradise, Hell, the layers of the heavens, the types of angels, and the species of animals in deserts, mountains, and seas. Without doubt, Jibril is more knowledgeable [؟] about them because he is longer-lived and has observed them more, so his knowledge of them is more abundant and complete. As for the transmitted sciences, which are only known through revelation, they were not attained by Muhammad, peace and blessings be upon him, nor by any other prophets except through Jibril, peace be upon him. So it is impossible for Muhammad to have a merit over Jibril in them. And as for Jibril, peace be upon him, he is the intermediary between God Almighty and all prophets, so he knows all past and present laws, and he also knows the laws and obligations of the angels. And Muhammad, peace and blessings be upon him, was not knowledgeable of any of that. Thus it is established that Jibril is more knowledgeable than Muhammad [؟], so it is necessary [؟] that he is superior to him, due to His statement: 'Are those who know equal to those who do not know?' [Quran 39:9]. The utmost that can be said is that Adam was taught the names, all of them, and the angels did not know them. But it is apparent that knowledge of realities and laws is superior to knowledge of names, so Jibril [؟] was superior to Adam [؟].
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage presents the twelfth proof for angels' superiority based on knowledge. Imam Razi first states the premise: the angel is more knowledgeable, and the more knowledgeable is superior. He supports the premise by noting that Jibril taught Muhammad (Quran 53:5), and a teacher must be more knowledgeable than the student. Then he divides knowledge into two categories: intellectual (aqli) and transmitted (naqli). For intellectual knowledge that is obligatory (like knowledge of God), angels and prophets are equal. For non-obligatory intellectual knowledge (like details of creation), Jibril has more because of longer lifespan and direct observation. For transmitted knowledge (revelation), all prophets receive it through Jibril, so Jibril knows all past and present laws and also the laws for angels, whereas Muhammad only knows what was revealed to him. Therefore Jibril is more knowledgeable, and by Quran 39:9, the knowledgeable are superior. He then addresses a counterargument: that Adam knew all names, but answers that knowledge of realities and laws is superior to knowledge of names, so Jibril is still superior.

Key Terms
الأعلم
العلوم العقلية
جبريل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main evidence Imam Razi gives for the claim that Jibril is more knowledgeable than Muhammad?
A) Jibril is older than Muhammad.
B) Jibril is an angel and angels are naturally more knowledgeable.
C) Jibril taught Muhammad, as indicated by the Quranic verse 'Severe in strength'.
D) Jibril knows all the names like Adam.

Key Takeaway
The principle that a teacher must know more than the student, combined with Jibril's longer life and observation, establishes his superiority in intellectual knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi argues that Jibril's longer life and direct observation make him more knowledgeable about creation. How does this challenge your assumption that revelation alone gives you all the knowledge you need? Are you actively seeking to learn from the vastness of God's creation through observation and reflection, or do you rely solely on books and lectures?
?
The teacher-learner principle implies that those who teach you know more than you in that subject. Who are your teachers—not just in formal settings, but in life? Do you have the humility to learn from those who have more experience, even if they are not considered 'religious scholars'?
?
Razi distinguishes between obligatory knowledge of God (which is equal for all) and non-obligatory knowledge of creation. In your own learning, are you neglecting the wonders of creation that can increase your awe of Allah? What specific aspect of creation will you study more deeply this week to grow your knowledge and worship?

Transmitted Knowledge and the Adam Counterargument
Razi discusses transmitted knowledge and counters the Adam objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why transmitted knowledge also shows Jibril's superiority over humans, and how Imam Razi refutes the objection that Adam knew all names.

Core Idea
Jibril knows all revelation and angelic laws, making him superior even in transmitted knowledge.

Having established Jibril's advantage in intellectual knowledge, Imam Razi turns to transmitted sciences — those known only through revelation. He argues that since Jibril is the intermediary for all prophets, he knows more than any single prophet. He then addresses a counterargument about Adam's knowledge of names, showing that knowledge of laws is superior.

Guided Path
1
Read the transmitted knowledge section
Read the Arabic passage from 'وأما العلوم النقلية' to the end. Understand how Razi treats transmitted knowledge.
2
Understand why Jibril knows more in transmitted sciences
Razi argues that all prophets receive revelation through Jibril, so Jibril knows all laws past and present, plus the laws of angels. Muhammad knows only what was revealed to him.
This makes Jibril's knowledge of transmitted sciences broader.
3
Examine the Adam objection
The objection: Adam was taught all names (Quran 2:31). Razi acknowledges this but argues that knowledge of realities and laws is superior to knowledge of names.
4
Conclude the proof
After the rebuttal, Razi's conclusion is that Jibril is superior to Adam as well. Reflect on how this completes the argument for angels' superiority over all humans.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Razi uses the fact that Jibril knows the laws of angels to argue for his superiority. Do you ever consider the obligations of beings beyond humanity? How does expanding your view of worship and obedience affect your own sense of responsibility?
?
If you deeply believed that an angel knows more about the purpose of your life than the best of humanity, would that change how you seek guidance? Whose knowledge do you trust most: that of a teacher, a scholar, or the one who brings revelation itself?

Jibril's Five Attributes of Perfection: The Thirteenth Proof
Imam Razi enumerates five attributes from Quran 69:40 to prove Jibril's superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses five attributes from Quran 69:40 to argue for Jibril's superiority over Prophet Muhammad.

Core Idea
Jibril's unique attributes in the Quran prove his supremacy over all prophets, including Muhammad.

Imam Razi presents his thirteenth proof for Jibril's superiority by analyzing a single Quranic verse. The verse describes a messenger (Jibril) with five explicit attributes of perfection. By examining each attribute, Razi builds a cumulative argument that Jibril holds a rank unmatched even by Muhammad.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and identify the attributes
Read Quran 69:40 and the list of five attributes. Notice how each attribute is introduced and what it emphasizes about Jibril's status.
Pay attention to the wording: 'رسول كريم' (noble messenger) sets the stage.
2
Understand the argumentative logic
See how each attribute implies uniqueness or superiority. Consider why being 'obeyed in the heavens' is especially significant for authority over angels.
Razi uses the principle that unrestricted praise indicates exclusivity.
3
Evaluate the conclusion
Reflect on why Razi thinks these attributes prove Jibril's superiority and how this relates to broader theological debates about the status of prophets and angels.
Compare with other proofs in the book for context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة عشرة : أنا نتكلم في حق جبريل ومحمد صلى الله عليه وسلم ونقول : إن جبريل أفضل من محمد، والدليل عليه قوله تعالى : ﴿إِنَّهُ لَقَوْلُ رَسُولٍ كَرِيمٍ﴾ [الحاقة : ٤٠] وقد وصف الله تعالى جبريل بستة من صفات الكمال :
أحدها : كونه رسولا من عند الله.
وثانيها : كونه كريما عند الله.
وثالثها : كونه ذا قوة عند الله، ومعلوم أن قوته عند الله لا تكون إلا قوته على الطاعات، وتخصيصه بالذكر في معرض المدح يدل على أن تلك القوة غير حاصلة لغيره.
ورابعها : كونه مكينا عند الله.
وخامسها : كونه مطاعا في عالم السماوات، وهذا يقتضي أن يكون مطاعا لكل الملائكة؛ لأن الإطلاق وعدم التقييد في معرض المدح يفيد ذلك.
The thirteenth proof: We speak concerning Jibril and Muhammad, peace and blessings be upon him, and we say that Jibril is superior to Muhammad, and the evidence for it is His statement: 'Indeed, it is the word of a noble messenger' [Quran 69:40]. And God described Jibril with six attributes of perfection: First: his being a messenger from God. Second: his being noble in the sight of God. Third: his being possessed of strength in the sight of God, and it is known that his strength in the sight of God is only his strength in acts of obedience, and his specific mention in the context of praise indicates that that strength is not possessed by others. Fourth: his being firmly established in the sight of God. Fifth: his being obeyed in the celestial realm, and this necessitates that he is obeyed by all angels because generality and lack of restriction in the context of praise indicates that.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage presents the thirteenth proof for Jibril's superiority over Muhammad. Imam Razi cites Quran 69:40, where God calls the Quran the word of a noble messenger (Jibril). He then enumerates five (or six) attributes of perfection that God has given Jibril: being a messenger, being noble, being strong (with strength in obedience), being firmly established (makin), and being obeyed in the heavens. The fifth attribute implies authority over all angels. The specific mention of these qualities in praise indicates that they are unique to Jibril or at least highlight his high rank. The argument is that these descriptors elevate Jibril above Muhammad, who is not described with such comprehensive attributes in the same context.

Key Terms
رسول
كريم
ذو قوة
مكن
مطاع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that the 'noble messenger' in Quran 69:40 refers to Prophet Muhammad.
Correction: Imam Razi clarifies that it refers to the angel Jibril, based on the context and the attributes that follow.

Checkpoint
Which Quranic verse does Imam Razi use as the basis for his thirteenth proof?
A) 69:40
B) 53:5
C) 2:285
D) 39:9

Key Takeaway
Jibril's five (or six) attributes of perfection in Quran 69:40 form a cumulative proof of his superiority over all prophets, including Muhammad.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Jibril's strength in God's sight is strength in obedience. What is one specific act of obedience you have been neglecting, and how will you build strength in it this week?
?
Jibril is 'firmly established' (makīn) in God's presence. In which area of your life do you feel most unstable in faith, and what concrete step can you take today to anchor yourself more firmly in Allah's command?
?
If Jibril's nobility comes from being a messenger who delivers God's words without alteration, how does your own transmission of Islamic knowledge—whether in speech or action—reflect or betray that same nobility? Will you check yourself before speaking about the deen from now on?

The Sixth Proof: Contrasting Descriptions of Jibril and Muhammad
Razi presents the sixth proof of angel superiority, with objection and refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's sixth proof for angels' superiority over humans, including his argument from the Quranic contrast between Jibril and Muhammad and his refutation of an objection regarding the identity of the 'noble messenger'.

Core Idea
Razi uses the contrast between Jibril's high attributes and Muhammad's simple description to prove angel superiority.

Razi presents a linguistic argument from the Quran itself. He contrasts the high attributes given to Jibril (trustworthiness, conveying revelation) with the mere negation of madness for Muhammad. The logic is that if Muhammad were equal, such a contrast would be belittling. He then anticipates an objection about the identity of the 'noble messenger' and refutes it. This passage is a complete dialectical unit.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof passage
Read the full Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice the flow from describing Jibril to describing Muhammad.
The passage includes both the proof and an objection-answer.
2
Understand the contrast argument
Identify the two sets of descriptions: Jibril's 'trustworthy in all acts of obedience' vs. Muhammad's 'not mad'. Why does Razi say this contrast would be disrespectful if they were equal?
3
Examine the objection
The objector suggests that the 'noble messenger' in verse 69:40 might refer to Muhammad. See how Razi refutes this by citing verse 81:23 which mentions seeing the angel.
This strengthens the proof by ensuring the high description is definitely about Jibril.
4
Reflect on the overall proof
Consider how the two parts work together: the first establishes the contrast, the second defends it against a potential misinterpretation. This is a typical kalam argument structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و سادسها : كونه أمينا في كل الطاعات وفي تبليغ وحي الله تعالى إلى الأنبياء. ثم إنه بعد أن وصف جبريل [؟] بهذه الصفات العالية وصف محمدا و يقوله : وما صَاحِبُكُم بِمَجْنُونِ ﴾ [التكوير : ٢٢] ولو كان محمد مساويا لجبريل في صفات الفضل أو مقارنة له لكان وصف محمد بهذه الصفة بعد وصف جبريل بتلك الصفات العالية غضا من منصب محمد ا وتحقيرا لشأنه، وذلك غير جائز، فدلت هذه الآية على أنه ليس لمحمد عند جبريل الله من المنزلة والقدرة والرتبة إلا أن يقال : إنه ليس بمجنون.

فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يكون قوله تعالى: ﴿ إِنَّهُ لَقَولُ رَسُولٍ كَرِيمٍ [الحاقة: ٤٠] صفة لمحمد لا لجبريل ال قلنا : لأن قوله : وَلَقَد رَوَاهُ بِالْأني الشينِ التكوير : ٢٣] يبطل ذلك.
And the sixth: that he is trustworthy in all acts of obedience and in conveying the revelation of Allah to the prophets. Then after he described Jibril with these high attributes, he described Muhammad saying: 'And your companion is not mad' (81:22). Had Muhammad been equal to Jibril in virtues or close to him, then describing Muhammad with this attribute after describing Jibril with those high attributes would be a belittlement of Muhammad's status and a degradation of his rank, and that is not permissible. So this verse indicates that Muhammad does not have before Jibril any station, power, or rank except to say that he is not mad.

If it is said: Why is it not permissible that His statement 'Indeed it is the word of a noble messenger' (69:40) is a description of Muhammad, not of Jibril? We say: Because His statement 'And he indeed saw him [Jibril] in the clear horizon' (81:23) nullifies that.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth proof for the superiority of angels over humans, specifically comparing Jibril and Muhammad. Razi argues that the Quran describes Jibril with high attributes of trustworthiness and conveying revelation, and then describes Muhammad simply as 'not mad'. If Muhammad were equal or close to Jibril, such a contrast would be disrespectful to Muhammad. Therefore, the verse shows that Muhammad's rank relative to Jibril is only that he is not mad – a lower description. An objection is raised that the verse 'Indeed it is the word of a noble messenger' (69:40) might refer to Muhammad, not Jibril. Razi refutes this by citing another verse (81:23) which specifies that the messenger saw Jibril in the clear horizon, confirming that the 'noble messenger' in 69:40 is Jibril, not Muhammad.

Key Terms
رسول كريم
صاحبكم بمجنون
جبريل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the 'noble messenger' in verse 69:40 refers to Muhammad.
Correction: Razi clarifies that verse 81:23, which mentions seeing the angel in the clear horizon, confirms that the messenger is Jibril, not Muhammad.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of Razi's sixth proof?
A) That Muhammad is equal to Jibril.
B) That describing Muhammad as 'not mad' after Jibril's high attributes proves the superiority of Jibril.
C) That the verse 'Indeed it is the word of a noble messenger' refers to Muhammad.
D) That angels are not superior to prophets.

Key Takeaway
Razi's sixth proof uses the Quran's sequential descriptions to argue that Jibril holds a higher rank than Muhammad, supported by refuting an alternative interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that the Prophet Muhammad — the best of creation — is described with only 'not mad' in contrast to Jibril's high attributes, what does that reveal about your own pride and self-perception? How will you internalize humility before the angels and prophets starting today?
?
Razi argues that even a slight praise of Muhammad after Jibril's attributes would be a belittlement. How often do you compare yourself to others, seeking to minimize their status? What concrete step will you take this week to recognize the true rank of others without envy or self-exaltation?
?
The objection Razi refutes shows people's tendency to misinterpret verses to suit their desires. In what area of your faith are you tempted to twist the clear meaning of revelation to avoid a hard truth? What will you do today to submit to the Quran's explicit wording, even if it's uncomfortable?

The Fifteenth Proof: The Argument from 'Many'
Razi presents the main proof from verse 17:70.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the word 'many' in the verse to argue that humans are not superior to angels.

Core Idea
The word 'many' implies a limit—humans are not superior to all creation.

Razi begins his fifteenth proof by closely examining the Quranic phrase 'over many of those We have created.' He reasons that if humans were superior to all creatures, the verse would have said 'over all' instead. This subtle linguistic point becomes a powerful argument for angelic superiority.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and find the main claim
Read the Arabic and translation. Identify the conclusion Razi draws from the verse.
The conclusion is that humans are not better than angels.
2
Understand the classification of creatures
Razi divides creatures into the morally responsible (mukallafun) and others, and further divides the morally responsible into four types.
The four types are angels, humans, jinn, and devils.
3
Follow the logical deduction
Trace Razi's reasoning: humans better than jinn and devils, but if also better than angels, then better than all. Yet the verse says 'many' not 'all'.
Notice the conditional: 'so if he were also better [than angels]...'
4
Consider why 'many' cannot be wasted
Razi asserts that the word 'many' would be superfluous if humans were superior to all. Then the verse would have said 'all' to be precise.
This relies on the Quranic principle that no word is meaningless.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة عشرة : قوله تعالى: ﴿وَفَضَّلْتَهُمْ عَلَى كَثِيرٍ مِّمَّنْ خَلَقْنَا تَفْضِيلا [الإسراء: ٧٠] ومخلوقات الله تعالى إما المكلفون أو ما عداهم، ولا شك ان المكلفين أفضل من غيرهم أما المكلفون فهم أربعة أنواع الملائكة والإنس والجن والشياطين، ولا شك أن الإنسي أفضل من الجن والشياطين، فلو كان أفضل أيضًا لزم أن يكون أفضل من جميع المخلوقات، فكان ينبغي أن يقول : من الملائكة

المسألة الثالثة والثلاثون
وفضلناهم على كل من خلقنا وعلى هذا التقدير يصير لفظ" كثير" ضائعاً، وذلك غير جائز، فعلمنا أنه ليس أفضل من الملك.

فإن قيل : هذا تمسك بدليل الخطاب وهو أن تخصيص الكثير بالذكر يدل على أن حال الباقي بخلافه، وأيضًا فهب أن جنس الملائكة أفضل من جنس البشر، لكن لا يلزم أن يكون كل فرد من أفراد هذا الجنس أشرف من من كل أفراد ذلك الجنس، وأيضا يجوز أن يكون المراد : وفضلناهم في الكرامة المذكورة في أول هذه الآية - وهي الكرامة في حسن الصورة ومزيد الذكاء والقدرة على الأعمال العجيبة. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : نحن نسلم أن البشر ليسوا أفضل من الملائكة في هذه الأمور، لكن لم قلتم : إنهم ليسوا أفضل منهم في كثرة الثواب ؟

قلنا : أما السؤال الأول فجوابه من وجهين :
أحدهما: هب أنه تمسك بدليل الخطاب، إلا انه حجة بدليل أن من قال : اليهودي إذا مات لا يبصر شيئًا فإنه يضحك من هذا الكلام لعلة أنه لما كان المسلم كذلك لم يبق لذكر اليهودي فائدة، وهذا يدل على أن تخصيص الشيء بالذكر يوجب نفي الحكم عما عداه. والثاني: أن هذا ليس تمسكًا بدليل الخطاب، بل هو تمسك بأنه لو كان البشر مفضلا على الكل لكان لفظ الكثير ضائعاً، ومعلوم أنه غير جائز. وأما السؤال الثاني فجوابه : أنا إنما نتمسك بهذه الآية أفضل من جنس البشر ، لا في بيان أحوال الأفراد، وإذا ثبت هذا التفاوت في الجنس كان الظاهر فضل الفرد على الفرد إلا عند إثبات التعارض.

وأما السؤال الثالث فجوابه : أن قوله: ﴿وَلَقَدْ كَرَّمْنَا بَنِي عَادَم [الإسراء: ۷۰] يتناول تكريمهم بالهداية والتوفيق للطاعة، فقوله : "وفضلناهم " يجب أن يكون عائدا إلى كل واحد من هذه الأحوال.
The fifteenth proof: His statement: 'And We have preferred them over many of those We have created, a great preference' (17:70). The creatures of Allah are either the morally responsible or others. There is no doubt that the morally responsible are better than others. As for the morally responsible, they are four types: angels, humans, jinn, and devils. There is no doubt that the human is better than jinn and devils. So if he were also better [than angels], then he would necessarily be better than all creatures, and then He should have said: 'over angels and We have preferred them over all whom We created.' On this estimation, the word 'many' would be wasted, and that is not permissible. So we know that he is not better than the angel.

If it is said: This is a use of the argument from the directive address (dalil al-khitab), which is that specifying 'many' indicates that the condition of the rest is different. Also, even if the species of angels is better than the species of humans, it does not necessarily follow that every individual of that species is more honorable than every individual of that other species. Also, it is possible that the intended meaning is 'We have preferred them in the honor mentioned at the beginning of this verse' – that is, honor in good form, increased intelligence, and ability for wondrous works. If this is established, then we say: we concede that humans are not better than angels in these matters, but why do you say they are not better than them in abundance of reward?

We say: As for the first question, its answer is from two perspectives:
One: Even if it is a use of the argument from directive address, it is a proof by the evidence that if someone says 'The Jew, when he dies, does not see anything,' it is laughable because since the Muslim is the same, there would be no benefit in mentioning the Jew. This indicates that specifying something by mention necessitates negating the ruling from what is other than it.
Second: This is not actually a use of the argument from directive address, but rather an argument that if humans were preferred over all, the word 'many' would be wasted, and that is not permissible.
As for the second question, its answer is: We only use this verse to show that the species of angel is better than the species of humans, not to show the states of individuals. And when this difference of species is established, the apparent [ruling] is that the individual is superior to the individual except when a countervailing [evidence] is established.
As for the third question, its answer is: His statement 'And indeed We have honored the children of Adam' (17:70) includes their honor by guidance and success in obedience. So His statement 'and We preferred them' must refer back to each of these states.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage presents the fifteenth proof for angelic superiority. Razi cites the verse 'We have preferred them over many of those We have created' (17:70) and analyzes it: Creatures are divided into morally responsible (mukallafun) and others; the morally responsible are better, and they consist of four types: angels, humans, jinn, and devils. Humans are better than jinn and devils, but not necessarily better than angels. If humans were better than all, the verse would have said 'over all' instead of 'over many', so the word 'many' indicates that humans are not superior to all creation, specifically not superior to angels. Three objections are raised: (1) This is an argument from 'dalil al-khitab' (the indication of the specific address), but that method is valid as shown by the example of the Jew. (2) Even if the species of angels is superior, individuals may vary. Razi responds: the argument is about species, not individuals; the default is that a typical individual of the superior species is superior unless countervailing evidence exists. (3) The preference might be limited to worldly honors like intelligence and ability, not reward. Razi answers: the earlier part of the verse mentions honoring children of Adam with guidance and success in obedience, so the preference should encompass these spiritual aspects as well.

Key Terms
كثير
مكلفون
تفضيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'many' simply means a large number and does not imply anything about angels.
Correction: Razi argues that in this context, 'many' is contrasted with 'all', so it indicates a limitation: humans are not preferred over everyone, specifically not over angels.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why does the use of the word 'many' in the verse indicate that humans are not superior to angels?
A) Because 'many' always includes angels.
B) Because if humans were superior to all creatures, the verse would say 'all' instead of 'many'.
C) Because 'many' refers only to humans.
D) Because the word 'many' is ambiguous.

Key Takeaway
The word 'many' in the verse restricts human superiority, proving that angels are superior in rank.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite Quran 17:70, do you really pause at the word 'كثير' and consider why Allah did not say 'all'? Where in your life have you taken Allah's precise wording for granted, assuming you know what it means without deeper reflection?
?
Razi argues that humans are not the best of creation. How does this truth confront your natural pride in being human? What specific thought or action must change today to align with this humility?
?
If angels are superior in rank, what does that say about your relationship with the unseen creation? Are you living as if you are the pinnacle, or are you striving to be as obedient as the angels who never tire in worship?

Objections and Responses in the Fifteenth Proof
Razi addresses three objections to his proof and provides responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three objections raised against Razi's proof and how he refutes each one.

Core Idea
Razi defends the argument from 'many' by addressing objections about wording, species, and scope.

Razi anticipates that his proof might be challenged. He presents three objections: one about the method of 'dalil al-khitab', one about individual vs. species superiority, and one about the nature of the preference. His detailed responses strengthen the original argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the three objections
Read the Arabic and translation from 'فإن قيل' onward. Identify the three objections clearly.
They are introduced by 'فإن قيل' and each starts with 'أما السؤال'.
2
Study the first objection and response
The first objection claims Razi is using dalil al-khitab. Razi gives two answers: (1) dalil al-khitab is valid, proven by the 'Jew dying' example; (2) Actually, this is not dalil al-khitab but an argument about wasted wording.
Notice the analogy: 'the Jew when he dies does not see anything' is laughable because it's pointless.
3
Study the second objection and response
The second objection says even if angel species is better, individuals may vary. Razi responds that the argument is about species, not individuals, and the default is that individuals of the superior species are superior unless counter-evidence exists.
4
Study the third objection and response
The third objection says the preference might be only worldly honors, not reward. Razi answers that the context of the verse (honoring children of Adam) includes spiritual matters like guidance and obedience.
He connects it to the beginning of the verse.

Source Text

وفضلناهم على كل من خلقنا وعلى هذا التقدير يصير لفظ" كثير" ضائعاً، وذلك غير جائز، فعلمنا أنه ليس أفضل من الملك.

فإن قيل : هذا تمسك بدليل الخطاب وهو أن تخصيص الكثير بالذكر يدل على أن حال الباقي بخلافه، وأيضًا فهب أن جنس الملائكة أفضل من جنس البشر، لكن لا يلزم أن يكون كل فرد من أفراد هذا الجنس أشرف من من كل أفراد ذلك الجنس، وأيضا يجوز أن يكون المراد : وفضلناهم في الكرامة المذكورة في أول هذه الآية - وهي الكرامة في حسن الصورة ومزيد الذكاء والقدرة على الأعمال العجيبة. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : نحن نسلم أن البشر ليسوا أفضل من الملائكة في هذه الأمور، لكن لم قلتم : إنهم ليسوا أفضل منهم في كثرة الثواب ؟

قلنا : أما السؤال الأول فجوابه من وجهين :
أحدهما: هب أنه تمسك بدليل الخطاب، إلا انه حجة بدليل أن من قال : اليهودي إذا مات لا يبصر شيئًا فإنه يضحك من هذا الكلام لعلة أنه لما كان المسلم كذلك لم يبق لذكر اليهودي فائدة، وهذا يدل على أن تخصيص الشيء بالذكر يوجب نفي الحكم عما عداه. والثاني: أن هذا ليس تمسكًا بدليل الخطاب، بل هو تمسك بأنه لو كان البشر مفضلا على الكل لكان لفظ الكثير ضائعاً، ومعلوم أنه غير جائز. وأما السؤال الثاني فجوابه : أنا إنما نتمسك بهذه الآية أفضل من جنس البشر ، لا في بيان أحوال الأفراد، وإذا ثبت هذا التفاوت في الجنس كان الظاهر فضل الفرد على الفرد إلا عند إثبات التعارض.

وأما السؤال الثالث فجوابه : أن قوله: ﴿وَلَقَدْ كَرَّمْنَا بَنِي عَادَم [الإسراء: ۷۰] يتناول تكريمهم بالهداية والتوفيق للطاعة، فقوله : "وفضلناهم " يجب أن يكون عائدا إلى كل واحد من هذه الأحوال.
The fifteenth proof: His statement: 'And We have preferred them over many of those We have created, a great preference' (17:70). The creatures of Allah are either the morally responsible or others. There is no doubt that the morally responsible are better than others. As for the morally responsible, they are four types: angels, humans, jinn, and devils. There is no doubt that the human is better than jinn and devils. So if he were also better [than angels], then he would necessarily be better than all creatures, and then He should have said: 'over angels and We have preferred them over all whom We created.' On this estimation, the word 'many' would be wasted, and that is not permissible. So we know that he is not better than the angel.

If it is said: This is a use of the argument from the directive address (dalil al-khitab), which is that specifying 'many' indicates that the condition of the rest is different. Also, even if the species of angels is better than the species of humans, it does not necessarily follow that every individual of that species is more honorable than every individual of that other species. Also, it is possible that the intended meaning is 'We have preferred them in the honor mentioned at the beginning of this verse' – that is, honor in good form, increased intelligence, and ability for wondrous works. If this is established, then we say: we concede that humans are not better than angels in these matters, but why do you say they are not better than them in abundance of reward?

We say: As for the first question, its answer is from two perspectives:
One: Even if it is a use of the argument from directive address, it is a proof by the evidence that if someone says 'The Jew, when he dies, does not see anything,' it is laughable because since the Muslim is the same, there would be no benefit in mentioning the Jew. This indicates that specifying something by mention necessitates negating the ruling from what is other than it.
Second: This is not actually a use of the argument from directive address, but rather an argument that if humans were preferred over all, the word 'many' would be wasted, and that is not permissible.
As for the second question, its answer is: We only use this verse to show that the species of angel is better than the species of humans, not to show the states of individuals. And when this difference of species is established, the apparent [ruling] is that the individual is superior to the individual except when a countervailing [evidence] is established.
As for the third question, its answer is: His statement 'And indeed We have honored the children of Adam' (17:70) includes their honor by guidance and success in obedience. So His statement 'and We preferred them' must refer back to each of these states.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage presents the fifteenth proof for angelic superiority. Razi cites the verse 'We have preferred them over many of those We have created' (17:70) and analyzes it: Creatures are divided into morally responsible (mukallafun) and others; the morally responsible are better, and they consist of four types: angels, humans, jinn, and devils. Humans are better than jinn and devils, but not necessarily better than angels. If humans were better than all, the verse would have said 'over all' instead of 'over many', so the word 'many' indicates that humans are not superior to all creation, specifically not superior to angels. Three objections are raised: (1) This is an argument from 'dalil al-khitab' (the indication of the specific address), but that method is valid as shown by the example of the Jew. (2) Even if the species of angels is superior, individuals may vary. Razi responds: the argument is about species, not individuals; the default is that a typical individual of the superior species is superior unless countervailing evidence exists. (3) The preference might be limited to worldly honors like intelligence and ability, not reward. Razi answers: the earlier part of the verse mentions honoring children of Adam with guidance and success in obedience, so the preference should encompass these spiritual aspects as well.

Key Terms
دليل الخطاب
جنس
فرد
تكرمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think dalil al-khitab is always invalid and that Razi's defense fails.
Correction: Razi shows that dalil al-khitab is valid with the Jew analogy, and also offers an alternative argument not dependent on it.

Checkpoint
What example does Razi use to defend the validity of dalil al-khitab?
A) The example of a Muslim dying.
B) The example of a Jew dying and not seeing anything.
C) The example of an angel dying.
D) The example of a prophet dying.

Key Takeaway
Razi's proof withstands three key objections by clarifying the method, scope, and context of the verse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi's first answer defends dalil al-khitab by giving an example about a Jew dying. Have you ever used a weak argument to defend a truth you know is correct, rather than seeking a stronger foundation? What would it take for you to abandon a weak proof and search for a robust one, even if it requires more effort?
?
Razi clarifies that the verse's preference includes spiritual rewards like guidance and success in obedience. Do you secretly believe that worldly success—intelligence, beauty, ability—is the true measure of honor? How does this lesson challenge your daily pursuit of honor?
?
The objection about species vs. individuals gives you an excuse to think that maybe you, as an individual, can be better than angels without being part of a superior species. Are you willing to accept that as a mere human, your only path to superiority over angels is through God's grace and obedience? Will you recommit to at least one act of obedience today to actualize that honor?

The Sixteenth and Seventeenth Proofs: Angels Superior by Explicit Quranic Statements
Razi cites two short proofs: the Prophet's denial of being an angel and the command to Adam not to eat lest he become an angel.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi uses two explicit Quranic verses to argue for the superiority of angels over humans, and why he notes that these proofs require subtle investigation.

Core Idea
Two Quranic verses explicitly indicate that the rank of angels is higher than that of humans.

Imam al-Razi continues his series of proofs for the superiority of angels by citing two clear Quranic statements. The first comes from Surah al-An'am, where the Prophet is instructed to disclaim being an angel, implying that being an angel is a higher station. The second comes from Surah al-A'raf, where Satan uses the prospect of becoming an angel as a temptation. These proofs are straightforward yet carry subtle implications that al-Razi hints at but does not elaborate here.

Guided Path
1
Read the two proofs
Read the Arabic text with translation to grasp the two arguments.
Notice the concise structure of each proof.
2
Analyze the implication of the first verse
Notice that the Prophet's denial of being an angel establishes that angelic status is considered higher.
The verse negates the claim of being an angel, not the reality.
3
Analyze the second verse
Observe how Satan's words imply that becoming an angel is a superior state.
Satan uses angelhood as a temptation, implying its desirability.
4
Consider the subtle investigations mentioned
Think about what deeper issues might be involved in these proofs, as al-Razi says there are subtle investigations.
This invites reflection on the nature of divine speech and inference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة عشرة : قوله تعالى : وقُل لَّا أَقُولُ لَكُمْ عِندِي خَزائن الله ولا اعلم الْغَيْبَ وَلَا أَقُولُ لَكُمْ إِنِّي مَلَكُ ﴾ [الأنعام : ٥٠] وهذا يدل على أن حال الملك أشرف. الحجة السابعة عشرة : قوله تعالى : مَا نَهَنكُمَا رَبُّكُمَا عَنْ هَذِهِ الشَّجَرَةِ إِلَّا أَن تَكُونَا ملكين [الأعراف: ۲۰] وهذا يدل على منصب الملك أشرف. ﴾

وفي هذين الدليلن أبحاث دقيقة.
The sixteenth proof: His statement: 'Say: I do not say to you that I possess the treasures of Allah, nor do I know the unseen, nor do I say to you that I am an angel' (6:50). This indicates that the state of the angel is more honorable. The seventeenth proof: His statement: 'Your Lord did not forbid you this tree except that you become angels' (7:20). This indicates that the station of the angel is more honorable.

And in these two proofs there are subtle investigations.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage presents two brief proofs for the superiority of angels. The sixteenth proof uses the verse where Allah instructs the Prophet to say that he does not claim to know the unseen or to be an angel, implying that being an angel is a higher state. The seventeenth proof uses the verse in which Satan tells Adam that the only reason Allah forbade the tree was to prevent them from becoming angels, which suggests that becoming an angel is a desirable and superior rank. Razi notes that there are subtle investigations (abhāth daqīqa) in these two proofs, indicating that they are not without complexity, though he does not elaborate here.

Correction: Razi argues the implication is clear: if the Prophet were an angel, he would have the higher status; by denying it, he acknowledges that angels are superior.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Two explicit Quranic verses serve as clear proofs for the superiority of angels over humans.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If becoming an angel is presented as a goal even in a deceptive whisper, what does that say about your own pursuit of spiritual elevation? Are you actively seeking to raise your rank with Allah, or are you content with mediocrity? What one step will you take today to increase your devotion?
?
The proofs show angels are superior to humans. Yet you are commanded to worship only Allah, not angels. Does this make you feel insignificant or motivated? How will you use this knowledge to increase your gratitude and strive for your own unique path to Allah's pleasure?

The Fifth Argument: Angels' Power Over the Physical World
Imam Razi presents the fifth argument for angels' superiority over humans.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's fifth argument for angels' superiority over humans, grounded in their observable control over natural phenomena.

Core Idea
Angels command nature; humans cannot.

Imam Razi presents a fifth proof for the superiority of angels, drawing from the observable world. He argues that angels possess a unique power over physical bodies—unlike human forces, it never tires. To illustrate, he points to the astonishing force of a tiny plant splitting rocks, attributing it to celestial power. Then he describes how angels directly govern winds, clouds, earthquakes, and more, citing Quranic verses. This argument positions angels as the active agents behind natural phenomena, far beyond human capacity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the full argument. Notice the logical flow from claim to analogy to scriptural evidence.
2
Identify the core claim
Imam Razi states that angels have power to change bodies and that this power is not subject to weariness. This is the foundation of the argument.
Contrast with human power that tires.
3
Examine the natural analogy
Consider the example of a weak plant sprout splitting rocks. Razi uses this to show that even a tiny organism can exert immense force due to celestial power flowing into it. If such power is present in a small thing, how much greater must the source be?
The plant is a conveyor, not the origin.
4
List the phenomena angels control
The passage enumerates winds, clouds, earthquakes, and celestial effects as acts of angels. Identify each and reflect on its scale.
These are observable, everyday events.
5
Connect to Quranic evidence
Razi quotes verses about 'distributors of command' and 'directors of command' to support his argument. Consider how these verses reinforce the idea of angelic agency.
The Quranic citations ground the argument in revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخامسها : الروحانيون لهم قدرة على تغيير الأجسام وتقليب الأجرام، والقدرة التي لهم ليست من جنس القوى المزاجية حتى يعرض لها كلال ولغوب، ثم إنك ترى الشظية الضعيفة من النبات في بدء نموها تفتق الأحجار وتشق الصخور، وما ذلك إلا بقوة فاضت عليها من جواهر القوى السماوية فما ظنك بتلك القوى السماوية ؟ فالروحانيون هم الذين يتصرفون في الأجسام السفلية تقليبًا وتصريفا، لا يستثقلون حمل الثقال ولا يستضعفون نقل الجبال، فالرياح تهب بتحريكاتها، والسحاب تعرض وتزول بتصريفاتها، والزلازل تطرأ بقوتها والآثار العلوية تحدث بمعونتها والكتاب الكريم ناطق بذلك، كما قال : فَالْمُقَيِّمَتِ أَمْرًا ﴾ [الذاريات : ٤] وقال : فالمديرات أمرًا ﴾ [النَّازعات : ٥] معلوم أن شيئًا من هذه الأحوال لا يصدر عن الأرواح البشرية، فأين أحدهما من الآخر ؟
And the fifth: The spiritual beings have power to change bodies and transform objects, and their power is not of the kind of temperamental forces that become weary and exhausted. Then you see the weak splinter of a plant at the beginning of its growth split rocks and break boulders, and that is only due to a power flowing upon it from the jewels of celestial forces, so what do you think of those celestial forces? The spiritual beings are those who dispose of lower bodies, turning and directing them, they do not find it heavy to carry heavy loads nor weak to move mountains. Winds blow by their movements, clouds appear and disappear by their dispositions, earthquakes occur by their strength, and celestial effects happen with their assistance, and the noble Book speaks of that, as He said: 'Then the distributors of command' [Adh-Dhariyat:4] and said: 'Then the directors of command' [An-Nazi'at:5]. It is known that none of these states issue from human spirits, so where is one compared to the other?
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument for the superiority of angels over humans. It is an argument from observable power. Imam Razi begins by stating that angels have the ability to change and transform physical bodies, and unlike human powers, their power is not subject to weariness or exhaustion. He then draws an analogy from nature: a weak plant sprout at its initial growth can split rocks and break boulders. This power is not inherent in the plant itself but flows from celestial forces (jawahir al-quwa al-samawiyya). If such a small thing can exhibit such power, then the celestial forces themselves must be immensely more powerful. Angels are precisely those spiritual beings who control the lower physical world: they direct the winds, clouds, earthquakes, and other atmospheric phenomena. The Quran confirms this in verses about the 'distributors of command' and 'directors of command.' Since humans cannot produce such effects, the conclusion is that angels are far superior. The argument uses empirical observation (the plant cracking rocks) combined with scriptural authority to establish a clear hierarchy.

Key Terms

تقليب الأجرام
القوى السماوية
المقيمات
المديرات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Angels are purely spiritual and have no physical effects.
Correction: This argument shows that angels actively govern physical phenomena like winds, clouds, and earthquakes.

Checkpoint

A) A rock falling from a mountain
B) A weak plant sprout splitting rocks
C) Wind blowing sand
D) Rain falling from clouds

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi uses observable natural phenomena and Quranic verses to prove that angels have a unique, tireless power over the physical world, establishing their superiority over humans.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If angels are constantly directing the winds, clouds, and earthquakes around you, how much do you truly rely on them as the unseen agents of Allah's will? When was the last time you consciously acknowledged that the gentle breeze or the sudden storm is under their command, and what did that do to your sense of security? Challenge yourself: before you sleep tonight, reflect on one natural phenomenon you witnessed today and ask Allah to increase your awareness of the angels who administer it.
?
The plant splits rocks not by its own power, but by a power flowing from the celestial forces. Consider your own achievements: the strength to work, the success in your studies, the love in your family. Are you claiming credit as if it were your own, or do you see the subtle hand of angelic assistance? If you truly believed that every good thing is facilitated by the angels ordered by Allah, how would your pride shatter and your gratitude intensify? Write down one success you had this week and consciously attribute it to the unseen support.
?
Imam Razi argues that human spirits cannot produce effects like controlling nature. Yet how often do you live as if you are the master of your environment, planning your life as if material causes alone determine outcomes? If you internalized that angels are the real movers behind the scenes, how would your fear of the future, your anxiety over weather, your worries about provision dissolve? This lesson demands a shift: will you continue to live as a materialist, or will you start conversing with the angels by acknowledging their role in your daily affairs?
